( ويَنْقُضُ مَسْحَ الخُفْ) أحد (أربعة أشياء: ) ، أولها : كلُّ شيءٍ يَنْقُضُ الوُضُوءَ)، لأنه بدل عن الغسل أو خلف، فينقضه ناقض أصله كالتيمم، وتقدم ) بيان النواقض. (وَ) الثاني: (نَزْعُ خُفُ) لسراية الحدث السابق إلى القدم، وهو الناقض في الحقيقة، وإضافة النقض إلى النزع مجاز، وإذا نزع واحداً لزمه قلع الآخر، لسراية الحدث ولزوم غسلهما، (وَلَو ) كان النزع (بخروج أكـثـر الـقــدم إلى ساق الخُفّ) في الصحيح؛ لأن حكم النزع يثبت بخروج القدم إلى ساق الخف؛ لمفارقة موضع المسح مكانه؛ فكان القدم قد ظهر، وحكم الأكثر كحكم الكل في الصحيح. وعن أبي حنيفة: أنـه إن خرج العقب أو أكثره إلى الساق، بطل المسح. وعن محمد أنه إن بقي في الخف من القدم قدر ما يجوز المسح عليه، لا ينتقض، وإلا انتقض. (و) الثالث: (إصابةُ المَاءِ أَكْثَرَ إِحْدَى القَدَمينِ في الخُفِّ، عَلَى الصَّحيح)، كما لو ابتل جميع القدم، فيجب قلع الخف وغسلهما، تحرزاً عــن الجمـع بين الغسل والمسح، ولذا لو تكلف وغسل رجليه من غير نزع الخف أجزأه عن الغسل، حتى لا يبطل بانقضاء المدة، وقال «الزاهدي»: لا ينتقض المسح ، وإن بلغ الماء الركبة. انتهى. والصحيح خلافه، كما علمته. (و) الرابع: (مُضِيُّ المُدَّةِ) للمقيم والمسافر، ولو في حال انصرافه ليتوضأ بسبق الحدث، فتبطل صلاته في الصحيح، فلا يبني، وإضافة النقض إلى المضي مجاز، والناقض في الحقيقة الحدث السابق لظهوره حينئذ، فإذا كان في الصلاة، وتمت مدته، ولم يجد ماء، قيل: يمضي على صلاته؛ لعدم الفائدة في نزعه؛ لكونه للماء ولا ماء، وقيل: تفسد لسريان الحدث، فيتيمم، وهذا وهو أشبه، والنقض بالمضي ( إنْ لم يَخَفْ ذهابَ رِجْلِهِ)، أو بعضها أو عطبها (من البرد)، فيجوز مسحه إذا خاف شيئاً منها، من غير توقيت بمدة، حتى يأمن على عضوه؛ لأن الضرر مدفوع، وظاهر إطلاق المتون؛ أن المسح بعد تمام المدة لخوف التلف، ولا يفارق صفته قبله، وفي «معراج الدراية وغيرها : لو مضت المدة، وهو يخاف البرد على رجله بالنزع، ويستوعبه بالمسح كالجبائر، (وبعد الثلاثة الأخيرة) وهي: نزع الخف، وابتلال أكثر القدم ومضي المدة، (غسل رجليه فقط)
It nullifies the wiping of the khuff four things: everything that nullifies wudu, removal of the khuff, even if by exiting most of the foot to the shaft of the khuff; reaching of water to most of one foot while in the khuff according to the correct opinion; and passing of the time limit, if he does not fear loss of his foot due to cold. And after the three latter, he washes only his two feet. (It nullifies the wiping of the khuff) one of (four things): the first: everything that nullifies wudu, because it is a substitute for washing or a replacement, so its nullifier nullifies it like tayammum, and the explanation of nullifiers has preceded. And the second: removal of the khuff, due to the transmission of the previous hadath to the foot, and it is the actual nullifier, and attributing nullification to removal is metaphorical. And if he removes one, he must remove the other, due to transmission of hadath and obligation of washing both. (Even if) the removal (is by exiting most of the foot to the shaft of the khuff) according to the correct opinion; because the ruling of removal is established by the foot exiting to the shaft of the khuff; due to the place of wiping leaving its place; so the foot has appeared, and the ruling of the majority is like the ruling of the whole according to the correct opinion. And from Abu Hanifa: that if the heel or most of it exited to the shaft, the wiping is invalidated. And from Muhammad: that if there remains in the khuff from the foot an amount that permits wiping upon it, it is not nullified, otherwise it is nullified. And the third: (reaching of water to most of one foot while in the khuff, according to the correct opinion), as if the entire foot becomes wet, then he must remove the khuff and wash both, as a precaution against combining washing and wiping, and therefore if he does extra work and washes his feet without removing the khuff, it suffices him from washing, such that it is not invalidated by the passing of the time limit. And al-Zahidi said: the wiping is not nullified even if water reaches the knee. End. And the correct opinion is contrary to that, as you have learned. And the fourth: (passing of the time limit) for the resident and the traveler, even if during [his time] for turning away to perform wudu due to preceding hadath, so his prayer becomes invalid according to the correct opinion, so he does not build upon it. And attributing nullification to passing is metaphorical, and the actual nullifier is the preceding hadath due to its appearance at that time. So if he is in prayer, and his time limit completes, and he does not find water, it is said: he continues upon his prayer; due to no benefit in removing it; because it is for water and there is no water. And it is said: it becomes invalid due to transmission of hadath, so he performs tayammum, and this is more appropriate. And nullification by passing (if he does not fear loss of his foot) or some of it or its destruction (due to cold), then it is permissible to wipe it if he fears anything from them, without limitation by a time limit, until he is safe for his limb; because harm is repelled. And the apparent meaning of the texts is that wiping after completion of the time limit due to fear of destruction does not differ from its description before it. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya and others: if the time limit passes while he fears cold on his foot by removal, and he wipes it entirely like splints. (And after the three latter) which are: removal of the khuff, wetting of most of the foot, and passing of the time limit, (he washes his feet only).
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah sharh of Nur al-Idah states and explains the four nullifiers of wiping over khuffs. The matn lists: (1) anything that nullifies wudu, because khuff wiping is a substitute for washing the feet, so its nullifier nullifies it just as tayammum is nullified; (2) removal of the khuff, because the previous hadath reaches the foot, and even if only one khuff is removed, the other must also be removed and both feet washed; exiting most of the foot to the shaft counts as removal; (3) water reaching most of one foot inside the khuff, as a precaution against combining washing and wiping; and (4) expiry of the prescribed time limit (one day and night for the resident, three for the traveler). The commentary clarifies intra-Hanafi differences: Abu Hanifa says if the heel or most of it exits, wiping is invalid; Muhammad says if enough of the foot remains to be wiped, it is not nullified. Regarding the time limit, if during prayer the time expires and no water is found, one view is to continue praying, another is to perform tayammum (the more appropriate view). If one fears harm from cold due to removing the khuff, the wiping continues indefinitely until safety. After any of the three latter nullifiers (removal, water entry, time expiry), only the feet are washed, not the rest of the wudu, because the previous hadath only affected the feet and the lost order (muwalat) is not a condition for wudu.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): Nullification or invalidation of the state of wiping.
خف (khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet, which may be wiped in wudu under conditions.
مسح (mash): Wiping; the act of passing a wet hand over the khuff instead of washing the foot.
المدة (al-muddah): The prescribed time limit for wiping over khuffs: one day and night for residents, three days and nights for travelers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Anything that nullifies wudu
B) Removing the khuff
C) Stepping in water without entering the khuff
D) Expiry of the time limit

Key Takeaway
The four nullifiers of khuff wiping are based on the principle that the substitute is tied to its original: anything that severs the substitute condition nullifies it.

The Sunnah Method of Wiping Over Khuffs
Statement of sunnah method and validity of alternatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah method of wiping over khuffs and distinguish between valid and sunnah-compliant wiping.

Core Idea
Wiping from toes to shin is sunnah; any other direction is valid but loses the sunnah.

This lesson focuses on the prescribed method of wiping over khuffs during wudu. The text presents both the recommended sunnah method and clarifies that alternate methods, while valid, do not earn the full reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah method
Read the text to find the recommended way of wiping: from the front of the khuffs (toes) to the base of the shin once, and separating between the toes.
The sunnah method maximizes reward and follows the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him).
2
Recognize permissible alternatives
The text states that wiping from shin to toes or sideways is valid (sahh). Understand that these alternatives fulfill the obligation but contradict the sunnah.
The key is that the purpose of wiping—wetting the khuff—is achieved regardless of direction.
3
Understand the fiqh consequence
The term 'sunnah' here indicates a recommended act. Contravening it does not invalidate the wiping but results in losing the reward of following the sunnah. In Hanafi fiqh, such an act is considered makruh tanzihan.
This distinction between validity (sihhah) and optimal practice (sunnah) is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراه من مقدم الخفين إلى أصل الساق مرة، وفرج بين أصابعه، فلو بدأ من الساق إلى الأصابع، أو مسح عليه عرضاً صح، لحصول المقصود، إلا أنه خالف السنة.
He wipes from the front of the two khuffs to the base of the shin once, and he separates between his toes. So if he begins from the shin to the toes, or wipes across it sideways, it is valid because the purpose is achieved, but he contradicts the sunnah.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Nur al-Idah instructs on the proper method of wiping over khuffs. The sunnah method is to start at the toes and wipe upwards to the ankle once, while also wiping between the toes. However, if one wipes from the top down or sideways, the wiping is still valid (sahih) because the essential purpose—wetting the khuff—is achieved. Such a method, though valid, contravenes the sunnah and is thus makruh tanzihan. This highlights the Hanafi principle that an obligatory act (here, wiping) performed in a non-sunnah manner still fulfills the obligation, but the sunnah is lost.

Key Terms
السنة (al-sunnah): The recommended method of wiping; contravening it does not invalidate the wiping but loses reward.
صح (sahha): Valid; the wiping legally suffices even if the sunnah method is not followed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Invalid because direction is wrong
D) Valid and sunnah is achieved

Key Takeaway
The sunnah method of wiping from toes to shin is recommended; any alternative direction is valid but misses the sunnah.

Prohibition of Wiping Over Turban, Cap, Veil, and Gloves
Ruling prohibiting wiping over headgear and hand coverings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping over headgear and hand coverings and explain the reasoning based on the exception of khuffs.

Core Idea
Wiping over anything other than khuffs is invalid because the permission for khuffs is a textual exception that cannot be extended by analogy.

This lesson addresses a common question in purification: can one wipe over a turban, cap, veil, or gloves instead of washing the actual body parts? The Hanafi school strictly limits the concession of wiping to leather socks (khuffs) alone. We will explore the legal reasoning behind this restriction and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited items
List the four items mentioned: turban (imamah), cap (qalansuwah), face-veil (burqu'), and gloves (qafazayn). Recognize that these cover the head, face, or hands.
The passage also defines these items lexically.
2
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The text states: 'because wiping over the khuff is established contrary to analogy, so nothing else is joined to it.' This means the allowance for khuffs is an exception to the general rule of washing; exceptions are not extended analogically.
This is a key usul principle in the Hanafi school.
3
Determine the consequence if already in wudu
If a person had wudu and subsequently wiped over these items (invalidating the wiping), they do not repeat the whole wudu. Only the feet must be washed because the hadath only affected the feet, and the loss of sequential order (muwalat) does not break wudu.
The passage clarifies this to avoid unnecessary repetition.
4
Recognize cross-madhhab difference
Note that this ruling is disputed; for example, the Shafi'i school allows wiping over part of the turban under certain conditions. The Hanafi position is more restrictive, relying on the principle that exceptions are not analogized.
This difference stems from different usul approaches to amr and istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ولا يجوز المسح على عِمَامَةٍ، وَقَلَنْسُوةٍ، وَبُرْقُع، وقُفَازَيْنِ) لأن المسح على الخف ثبت بخلاف القياس، فلا يلحق به غيره. وليس عليه إعادة بقية الوضوء إذا كان متوضئاً، لأن الحدث السابق هو الذي حل بقدميه، وقد غسل بعده سائر الأعضاء، وبقيت القدمان فقط بلا غسل الآن حكماً، لسريان الحدث إليهما، فلا يجب إلا غسلهما، لا غسل الأعضاء المغسولة ثانياً، لأن الفائت الموالاة، وهي ليست شرطاً في الوضوء. (القفاز بالضم والتشديد: شيء يعمل لليدين محشواً بقطن، له أزرار تزر على الساعدين من البرد، تلبسه النساء، ويتخذه الصياد من جلد ولبد، يغطي به الكف والأصابع، اتقاء مخاليب الصقر، والقلنسوة بفتح القاف وضم السين، والبرقع بضم الباء الموحدة، وسكون الراء المهملة، وضم القاف وفتحها: خرقة تثقب للعينين، تلبسها الدواب، ونساء الأعراب على وجوههن.)
(It is not permissible to wipe over a turban, a cap, a face-veil, and two gloves) because wiping over the khuff is established contrary to analogy, so nothing else is joined to it. And he is not required to repeat the rest of the wudu if he was in a state of wudu, because the previous hadath is the one that reached his feet, and he has washed the other limbs after that, and only the two feet remain without washing now by hukum, due to the transmission of the hadath to them, so only washing them is obligatory, not washing the already washed limbs again, because what was lost is the order (muwalat), and it is not a condition in wudu. (Al-qafaz: with dammah and tashdid: something made for the hands stuffed with cotton, having buttons that are buttoned on the forearms due to cold, women wear it, and the hunter takes it from leather and felt, covering the palm and fingers, to protect from the talons of the falcon. Al-qalansuwah: with fatha on the qaf and dammah on the sin. Al-burqu: with dammah on the ba and sukun on the ra, and dammah or fatha on the qaf: a cloth pierced for the eyes, worn by animals and Bedouin women on their faces.)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage prohibits wiping over items other than khuffs, specifically: the turban (imamah), cap (qalansuwah), face-veil (burqu'), and gloves (qafazayn). The reasoning is that wiping over khuffs is an exception to the general rule that only washing suffices; thus analogical extension to other coverings is impermissible. The text further clarifies that if a person had already performed wudu and then these items were removed or became invalid, he does not need to repeat the washing of other limbs—only the feet must be washed. This is because the hadath only affected the feet, and the loss of sequential order (muwalat) is not a condition for wudu validity. The passage ends with lexical definitions of these items, noting their materials and uses.

Key Terms
عمامة (imāmah): A headwrap wound around the head; not permissible to wipe over.
قلنسوة (qalansuwah): A cap or hood worn on the head; wiping over it is invalid.
برقع (burqu'): A face veil with openings for eyes; wiping over it is invalid.
قفاز (qafaz): Gloves that cover the hands and fingers; wiping over them invalid.
موالاة (muwalat): Sequential order in wudu; not a condition for validity according to this passage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts the concession of wiping over coverings solely to khuffs, and any extension to other head or hand coverings is invalid.

Ruling on Wiping over Splints and Bandages (Jabirah)
Ruling on wiping over medical bandages with proofs and comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wiping over a splint (jabirah), identify its legal nature (asl vs badal), and apply the distinctions between jabirah and khuff in subsidiary rulings.

Core Idea
Wiping over a splint is like washing (ka-ghusl) and is an original ruling (asl), not a substitute (badal) like khuff.

When a limb is bandaged due to injury or surgery, the obligation of washing or wiping during wudu is modified by necessity. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on wiping over splints (jabirah) and medical bandages, explains why it is treated as an original duty rather than a substitute, and highlights the practical differences from wiping over leather socks (khuff).

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering condition
The ruling applies when a person is bled, wounded, or has a broken limb, and they cannot wash the limb (due to harm) and cannot wipe the skin directly (e.g., under the bandage).
The inability may be actual or due to risk of harm.
2
State the basic ruling
Wiping over the majority of the splint/bandage is wajib (obligatory) once. The Hanafi mu'tamad is that it is not fard, but wajib, and one wipe suffices.
The proof is from ahad hadith, so the level of obligation is wajib, not fard.
3
Examine the proofs
The author cites multiple hadith: the Prophet (salla Allahu alayhi wa sallam) wiped over his bandage during wudu after being wounded at Uhud, and he ordered Ali to wipe over a splint.
These narrations show that wiping over the bandage is the established practice.
4
Understand the legal nature: asl (original) vs badal (substitute)
The text emphasizes that wiping over jabirah is like washing (ka-ghusl) and is an original ruling (asl), not a substitute (badal) like khuff. This difference drives many subsidiary rulings.
Because it is an original ruling, it is not limited by time, and it does not require prior purity when tying the splint.
5
Apply the ten distinctions between jabirah and khuff
The passage lists several differences: no time limit, no condition of purity when tying, allows mixing with washing, not invalidated by falling off before recovery, etc. Work through each distinction and understand the underlying logic.
These distinctions all flow from the fundamental difference between asl and badal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في حكم الجبيرة ونحوها
إذا افتصد، أو جرح، أو كسر عضوه، فشده بخرقة، أو جبيرة، وكان لا يستطيع غسل العضو، ولا يستطيع مسحه، وجب المسح على أكثر ما شد به العضو، وكفى المسح على ما ظهر من الجسد بين عصابة المفتصد والمسح كالغسل.
( إذا افتصِدَ، أو جُرِحَ، أو كُسِرَ عُضوهُ، فشدّه بخِرْقَةٍ لهما (أو جبيرة) هي: عيدان تلف بخرق أو ورق وتربط على العضو المنكسر، (وَكَانَ لا يَسْتَطِيعُ غَسَلَ العُضو)، ولو بالماء الحار، فإنه إذا قدر عليه، ولا يضره الحار، يجب استعماله، وقيل: لا يجب. (ولا يستطيعُ مَسْحَهُ، وجَبَ المَسْحُ) على الصحيح، عن أبي حنيفة وبه قالا : مرة واحدة، وهو الصحيح. وقيل: يكرر إلا في الرأس. واستحباب المسح رواية. وقيل: فرض، والصحيح من مذهبه، أنه ليس فرضاً وعليه عليه وسلم كان يمسح على الجبَائِرِ »، و « توضأ ابنُ عمر وكفّه معصوبة، فمسح عليها، وعلى العصائب، وغسل ما ذلك» ). و « لما كسر زند علي رضي الله عنه يوم أحد، أو يوم خيبر أمره النبي أن يمسح على الجبائر ) ويمسح عَلَى أَكْثَرِ مَا شَدَّ بِهِ العُضَ)، هو الصحيح، لئلا يؤدي إلى فساد الجراحة بالاستيعاب، (وكفى المسح على ما ظهر من الجسد بين عِصابة المُفْتَصدِ)، ونحوه إن ضره حلها، تبعاً للضرورة، لأنه إذا غسل الفرجة يسري الماء إلى الجرح فيضره، ومن ضرورة الحل أن يكون في مكان لا يقدر على ربطها بنفسه، ولا يجد من يربطها، ولا فرق بين الجراحة، والقرحة، والكي والكسر، وإن لم يضره الحل حلها، وغسل الصحيح الذي تحتها، ومسح الجرح، وإن ضره مسح على الخرقة، لأن الضرورة تقدر بقدرها (والمَسْحُ) على الجبيرة، وخرقة الجراحة ( كَالغَسل) ، لما تحتها وليس بدلاً، بخلاف المسح على الخف، لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام : « أمر علياً بالمسح على الجبيرة في إحدى يديهِ » ، فثبت أن المسح على الجبيرة ما دام العذر قائماً أصل لا بدل، ولإن كان في نفسه فلا يتوقتُ بجدة. ولا يُشترط شد الجبيرة على طهر. ويجوز مسح جبيرة إحدى الرجلين مع غسل الأخرى. ولا يَبْطُلُ المَسْحُ بسُقُوطِهَا قبلَ البُرْء. ويجوز تبديلها بغيرها، بخلاف مسح بدلاً، بدليل أنه لا يجوز عند القدرة على الغسل، لكن نُزِّلَ منزلة الأصل، لعدم القدرة على الأصل، الخف، فإنه لا يُعطى له حكم الغسل، بل هو بدل محض، للقدرة على الأصل، فلذا فارق المسحُ على الجبيرة مسح الخف في مسائل ذكرها بقوله: (فلا يتوقتُ) مسح الجبيرة ونحوهــا (بمدة) لأنه أصل، فما دام العذر قائماً مسح عليها، بخلاف الخف، فإنه مؤقت بمدة، (ولا يُشترط) لصحة المسح (شدُّ الجبيرةِ) ونحوها ( عَلَى طُهر)، فيجوز مسحها، ولو شدت حال الجنابة والحدث، لأن في اشتراط الطهارة في تلك الحالة حرجاً وهو مدفوع ولأنَّ غسل ما تحتها، قد سقط وانتقل إليها، بخلاف الخف. (ويجوز مسح جبيرة إحدى الرجلين مع غسل الأخرى)؛ لكونه أصلاً، كمـا ذكـرنـا، بخلاف الخف، فلا يمسح على خف، ويغسل الرجل الأخرى؛ لكونه بدلاً، فلا يُجمـــع مــع المبدل، ( ولا يَبْطُلُ المَسْحُ على الجبيرة ونحوها ، (بسُقُوطِهَا قبل البُرْء)؛ لـقـيـام الـعـذر المبيح للمسح، بخلاف الخف، فإنه يبطل مسحه، لكون الخف مانعاً سراية الحدث، فإذا فارق المحـل سـرى إليـه الحدث، وإذا سقطت عن برء بإحدى رجليه وهو متوضئ لا يلزمه غير غسل محلها، بخلاف الخف إذا نزع عن رجل لزمه نزع الأخرى وغسلهما، ويستوي فيها الحدث الأكبر والأصغر، بخلاف الخف، فإنه لا يجوز المسح عليه في الأكبر، ويجب استيعابها في رواية كما قدمناه، بخلاف الخف، فإنه لا يجب استيعابه، رواية واحدة، ويجوز مسح العصابة العليا بعد مسح السفلي، وإذا زالت العصابة العليا، وقد مسح عليها بعد شد السفلى، لا يعيد المسح عليها ، بخلاف الخـف فـوق خف)، ولا يبطل مسحها بابتلال ما تحتها، بخلاف الخف، ويجوز تبديلها بِغَيْرِهَا) بعد مسحها
Chapter on the ruling of the splint and similar things. When one is bled, or wounded, or his limb is broken, and he ties it with a cloth or a splint, and he cannot wash the limb, nor can he wipe it, wiping is obligatory over the majority of what he tied the limb with, and wiping over what appears of the body between the bandage of the one bled suffices, and wiping is like washing. (If he is bled, or wounded, or his limb is broken, and he ties it with a cloth for both) (or a splint, which is: sticks wrapped in cloth or paper and tied on the broken limb), (and he cannot wash the limb), even with hot water, for if he is able to do so and hot water does not harm him, he must use it, and it is said: not obligatory. (And cannot wipe it, wiping is obligatory) according to the correct view from Abu Hanifa, and the two said: once, and that is correct. And it is said: repeated except on the head. And recommended wiping is a narration. And it is said: obligatory, and the correct view of his madhhab is that it is not obligatory, and reliance is upon it. The basis for it is what is from Abu Umamah that the Prophet, when Ibn Qumiah shot him on the day of Uhud, 'I saw the Prophet, when he performed wudu, he untied his bandage and wiped over it with wudu.' And what is from Ibn Umar: 'The Prophet used to wipe over splints.' And 'Ibn Umar performed wudu while his palm was bandaged, so he wiped over it and over the bandages and washed what was...' And 'When Ali's forearm was broken on the day of Uhud or the day of Khaybar, the Prophet ordered him to wipe over the splints.' And he wipes over the majority of what he tied the limb with, that is the correct view, so as not to lead to spoiling the wound by covering it entirely. (And wiping over what appears of the body between the bandage of the one bled suffices) and similar things if untying harms him, following necessity, because if he washes the gap, water will seep into the wound and harm him. And from the necessity of untying is that he is in a place where he cannot tie it himself and does not find someone to tie it. And there is no difference between wound, ulcer, cautery, and fracture. And if untying does not harm him, he unties it and washes the healthy part beneath it and wipes the wound. And if it harms him, he wipes over the cloth, because necessity is measured by its degree. (And wiping) over the splint and the wound cloth (is like washing) for what is beneath it and is not a substitute, unlike wiping over the khuff, because he ordered Ali to wipe over the splint on one of his hands, so it is established that wiping over the splint as long as the excuse exists is an original ruling, not a substitute, even though in itself it is not timed. And tying the splint is not required upon purity. And it is permissible to wipe the splint of one foot while washing the other. And wiping is not invalidated by its falling before recovery. And it is permissible to replace it with another, unlike wiping as a substitute, evidence being that it is not permissible when able to wash, but it is given the status of the original due to inability to perform the original. The khuff, however, is not given the ruling of washing, rather it is a pure substitute due to ability to perform the original. Therefore, wiping over the splint differs from wiping over the khuff in issues he mentioned: (It is not timed) wiping over the splint and the like (by a period) because it is original, so as long as the excuse exists, he wipes over it, unlike the khuff, which is timed by a period. (And it is not required) for the validity of wiping (tying the splint) and the like (upon purity), so it is permissible to wipe it even if tied during janabah and hadath, because requiring purity in that state is hardship, and hardship is repelled, and because washing what is beneath it has fallen and transferred to it, unlike the khuff. (And it is permissible to wipe the splint of one foot while washing the other) because it is original, as we mentioned, unlike the khuff, so he does not wipe over a khuff while washing the other foot, because it is a substitute, so it is not combined with the substituted. (And wiping over the splint and the like is not invalidated) (by its falling before recovery) due to the existence of the excuse that permits wiping, unlike the khuff, for its wiping becomes invalid because the khuff prevents the transmission of hadath, so when it leaves the place, hadath reaches it. And if it falls off after recovery on one of his feet while he is in wudu, he is only required to wash its place, unlike the khuff: if it is removed from one foot, he must remove the other and wash both. And major and minor hadath are equal in it, unlike the khuff, on which wiping is not permissible for major hadath. And covering the whole [in wiping] is required in one narration as we mentioned, unlike the khuff, for which covering the whole is not required, in one narration. And it is permissible to wipe the upper bandage after wiping the lower. And if the upper bandage is removed after he wiped it while the lower was tied, he does not repeat wiping over it, unlike the khuff on top of a khuff. And his wiping is not invalidated by wetness reaching what is beneath it, unlike the khuff. And it is permissible to replace it with another after wiping it.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage from Imdad al-Fattah, a commentary on Nur al-Idah, discusses the rulings (ahkam) of wiping over a splint (jabirah) and similar medical coverings for wounds, fractures, or incisions. The author begins by stating the basic ruling: if a person has a wound or fracture and ties it with a cloth or splint, and they cannot wash the limb (due to harm) and also cannot wipe the area directly (due to pain or risk), then they must wipe over the majority of the wrapping. The commentary expands that this applies to cases like phlebotomy, wounds, or fractures, and defines 'jabirah' as sticks wrapped in cloth. It notes a dispute: if hot water is available and does not harm, it must be used (some say not). The correct Hanafi position is that wiping once is sufficient, but some say it must be repeated except on the head. The wiping is not obligatory (fard) but wajib; reliance is on this. The proof comes from several hadith: the Prophet untied his bandage and wiped over it during wudu; he also wiped over splints; Ibn Umar did similarly; and he ordered Ali to wipe over a splint. The commentary emphasizes that the wiping is like washing (ka-ghusl) for the area beneath, not a substitute (badal), unlike the khuff (leather socks). As long as the excuse exists, the ruling remains original (asl). Therefore, unlike khuff, wiping over jabirah is not limited by time or condition of prior purity. Several differences are listed: (1) No time limit; (2) Not requiring purity when tying; (3) One can wipe a splint on one foot while washing the other; (4) The wiping is not invalidated if the splint falls before recovery; (5) Replacing the splint after wiping is allowed; (6) Covering the whole splint is required in one narration; (7) Major and minor hadath are equal; (8) Wiping over an upper bandage after the lower is allowed; (9) If the upper falls, no need to re-wipe the lower; (10) Wetness under the splint does not invalidate. These distinctions all arise because wiping over jabirah is considered like washing the wound itself, due to necessity (darurah).

Key Terms
جبيرة (jabirah): Sticks wrapped in cloth or paper tied on a broken limb to set it.
عصابة (isabah): Bandage or wrapping used for a wound or phlebotomy.
فصد (fasd): Phlebotomy: opening a vein to let out blood for medical treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, what is the status of wiping over a splint (jabirah) compared to wiping over khuff?
A) Both are substitutes (badal).
B) Wiping over jabirah is like washing (ka-ghusl) and is an original ruling, while khuff is a substitute.
C) Both are original rulings.
D) Wiping over jabirah is not permissible.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over a splint is an original obligation (asl) necessitated by inability to wash, differing fundamentally from the substitute wiping over khuff.

Differences Between Wiping Over Splints (Jabirah) and Khuff: Leniencies for Necessity
Detailed commentary contrasting five rules of jabirah with khuff based on the principle of asl vs badl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five key Hanafi rulings that distinguish wiping over a splint (jabirah) from wiping over khuff, and explain the underlying principle of jabirah being treated as an original (asl).

Core Idea
Jabirah is treated as the limb itself (asl), giving leniencies; khuff is a pure substitute (badl mahd) with stricter rules.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah explains the Hanafi rulings on wiping over a splint (jabirah) during wudu or ghusl. The commentator systematically contrasts five specific rules of jabirah with those of khuff (leather socks). Understanding these differences is crucial because they arise from a fundamental principle: jabirah is considered an extension of the limb due to necessity, whereas khuff is a mere substitute. This lesson will guide you through those differences and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational principle
The commentary states that jabirah is like an original (asl) while khuff is a pure substitute (badl mahd). This principle drives the differences.
2
List the five differences mentioned
Work through each one: (a) no time limit, (b) no requirement of purity when applying, (c) can wipe one foot and wash the other, (d) wipe not invalidated by falling off before healing, (e) can replace without repeating wipe.
The passage also notes a sixth difference regarding major/minor hadath.
3
For each difference, note the Hanafi reasoning
For example, no time limit because necessity persists; no need for purity to avoid hardship; mixing wipe and wash is allowed because jabirah is like the limb itself, etc.
Each rationale is explicitly or implicitly connected to the asl/badl distinction.
4
Contrast with khuff rules
Explain why each difference exists by referring to the substitute status of khuff: time limit because it's a license, must be applied in purity, cannot combine wipe and wash, removal invalidates wipe, etc.
The contrast is direct: 'bikhilaf al-khuff' appears for each point.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on isti'ab
The passage mentions that covering all or most of the splint is required in one narration but not required (or not mentioned) for khuff. This shows variation within the school.
Later preferred position is to require covering the majority.

فلا يتوقتُ بجدة. ولا يُشترط شد الجبيرة على طهر. ويجوز مسح جبيرة إحدى الرجلين مع غسل الأخرى. ولا يَبْطُلُ المَسْحُ بسُقُوطِهَا قبلَ البُرْءِ. ويجوز تبديلها بغيرها. بخلاف مسح الخف، فإنه لا يعطى له حكم الغسل، بل هو بدل محض، للقدرة على الأصل، فلذا فارق المسحُ على الجبيرة مسح الخف في مسائل ذكرها بقوله: (فلا يتوقتُ) مسح الجبيرة ونحوها (بمدة) لأنه أصل، فما دام العذر قائماً مسح عليها، بخلاف الخف، فإنه مؤقت بمدة، (ولا يُشترط) لصحة المسح (شدُّ الجبيرةِ) ونحوها (على طهر)، فيجوز مسحها، ولو شدت حال الجنابة والحدث، لأن في اشتراط الطهارة في تلك الحالة حرجاً وهو مدفوع ولأنَّ غسل ما تحتها، قد سقط وانتقل إليها، بخلاف الخف. (ويجوز مسح جبيرة إحدى الرجلين مع غسل الأخرى)؛ لكونه أصلاً، كما ذكرنا، بخلاف الخف، فلا يمسح على خف ويغسل الرجل الأخرى؛ لكونه بدلاً، فلا يُجمع مع المبدل، (ولا يَبْطُلُ المَسْحُ على الجبيرة ونحوها (بسُقُوطِهَا قبل البُرْء)؛ لقيام العذر المبيح للمسح، بخلاف الخف، فإنه يبطل مسحه، لكون الخف مانعاً سراية الحدث، فإذا فارق المحل سرى إليه الحدث، وإذا سقطت عن برء بإحدى رجليه وهو متوضئ لا يلزمه غير غسل محلها، بخلاف الخف إذا نزع عن رجل لزمه نزع الأخرى وغسلهما، ويستوي فيها الحدث الأكبر والأصغر، بخلاف الخف، فإنه لا يجوز المسح عليه في الأكبر، ويجب استيعابها في رواية كما قدمناه، بخلاف الخف، فإنه لا يجب استيعابه، رواية واحدة، ويجوز مسح العصابة العليا بعد مسح السفلي، وإذا زالت العصابة العليا وقد مسح عليها بعد شد السفلى، لا يعيد المسح عليها، بخلاف الخف فوق خف، ولا يبطل مسحها بابتلال ما تحتها، بخلاف الخف، ويجوز تبديلها بغيرها بعد مسحها، ولا يجب إعادة المسح عليها، والأفضل إعادته.
It is not limited by a period. It is not required to apply the splint in a state of purity. It is permissible to wipe over a splint on one foot while washing the other. Wiping does not become invalid by its falling off before healing. It is permissible to replace it with another. Contrary to wiping over the khuff, for it is not given the ruling of washing, but is a pure substitute due to ability to use the original. Therefore wiping over the splint differs from wiping over the khuff in matters mentioned by his saying: 'It is not limited by a period' for wiping over the splint and its like, because it is an original, so as long as the excuse exists he wipes over it, contrary to the khuff which is limited by a period. 'It is not required for validity of wiping that the splint be applied in a state of purity', so it is permissible to wipe over it even if applied during janabah or hadath, because requiring purity at that time would be hardship and is repelled, and because washing underneath it has fallen and transferred to it, contrary to the khuff. 'It is permissible to wipe over a splint on one foot while washing the other' because it is an original, as we mentioned, contrary to the khuff; he may not wipe over a khuff and wash the other foot because it is a substitute, so it is not combined with the substituted. 'Wiping over the splint and its like does not become invalid by its falling off before healing' due to the existence of the excuse permitting wiping, contrary to the khuff, for its wiping becomes invalid because the khuff prevents transmission of hadath, so when it leaves the place, hadath transmits to it. If it falls off after healing on one of his feet while he is in wudu, nothing else is required but washing its place, contrary to the khuff: if it is removed from one foot, he must remove the other and wash both. For the splint, major and minor hadath are equal, contrary to the khuff, for wiping it is not permitted in major hadath. Covering all or most of it is required in one narration, as we presented, contrary to the khuff, for covering all is not required, in one narration. It is permissible to wipe over the upper bandage after wiping the lower one. If the upper bandage is removed and he had wiped over it after tightening the lower, he does not repeat the wipe over it, contrary to khuff over khuff. Its wipe is not invalidated by wetness of what is under it, contrary to the khuff. It is permissible to replace it with another after wiping it, and it is not obligatory to repeat the wipe over it, but it is better to repeat it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof commentary on the matn of Nur al-Idah concerning the rules of wiping over splints (jabirah) compared to wiping over leather socks (khuff). The commentator explains five key differences based on the principle that jabirah is treated as an original (asl) while khuff is a pure substitute (badl mahd). First, there is no time limit for jabirah; it remains valid as long as the excuse persists, unlike khuff which has a fixed duration. Second, it is not required to apply the splint in a state of purity; even if applied during janabah, wiping is valid, because requiring purity would cause hardship. Third, one may wipe over a splint on one foot while washing the other foot—this is permitted because jabirah is like the original limb. Fourth, if the splint falls off before healing, the wipe does not become invalid because the excuse still exists. Fifth, one may replace the splint after wiping without needing to repeat the wipe, though repeating is better. The commentary also notes that covering all or most of the splint is required in one narration, and that major and minor hadath are treated equally for jabirah, unlike khuff which cannot be wiped in major hadath. The overall Hanafi position is that the rules for jabirah are more lenient due to necessity.

Key Terms
الجبيرة (al-jabirah): A splint or bandage applied to a broken bone or wound that prevents washing the affected area.
الخف (al-khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet; allowed to wipe over in wudu under certain conditions.
البدل (al-badl): A substitute: something that stands in for another act or object in ritual purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding the duration of wiping over a splint (jabirah)?
A) It is valid only for one day and night like khuff.
B) It is valid as long as the excuse exists, with no fixed time limit.
C) It is valid only for three days.
D) It becomes invalid after the first use.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, wiping over a splint is a necessity-based rule that grants more flexibility than wiping over khuff, because the splint is treated as the limb itself (asl).

Wiping Over Coverings and Intention: Necessity and Proof
Rulings on necessity-based wiping and intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when wiping is permitted for eye and nail coverings and apply the Hanafi rule that intention is not required for wiping over khuff, jabirah, and head.

Core Idea
Necessity permits wiping over coverings; intention is not required for water-based purification like wiping.

In this lesson, we examine two related issues from the Hanafi school: when necessity (darurah) allows wiping over a covering instead of washing, and whether intention (niyyah) is required for wiping over the khuff, splint, and head. The text presents concrete examples of eye inflammation and a broken nail, then tackles the debated point about intention, providing the school's relied-upon position and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cases of necessity
Locate the specific conditions (ramad, broken nail, medicine or covering) and the ruling that wiping is permitted if washing or removing the covering would cause harm.
Note the principle: necessity is measured by its extent.
2
Extract the ruling on intention
Find the statement that intention is not required for wiping over khuff, jabirah, and head. Contrast it with the alternative view that requires intention by analogy to tayammum.
The text says 'al-azhar' (the more apparent view) indicates the preferred position.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Understand the proof: wiping is water purification (taharah bi al-ma') like wudu, of which it is a part. Tayammum is different because it is dry purification with specific evidence requiring intention.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on analogy with wudu.
4
Note the intra-madhhab dispute
Recognize that some Hanafi scholars argued for intention by analogy to tayammum, but the relied-upon view rejects that because the analogy fails due to the water-based nature of wiping.
The dispute is resolved by preferring the analogy to wudu over the analogy to tayammum.
5
Apply the ruling to new scenarios
Practice applying the rule: for any covering placed on a wound due to harm from water, wiping is allowed; intention is not required for any wiping that is part of wudu (khuff, jabirah, head).
Test your understanding by deriving the ruling for a bandage on a cut that cannot be removed.

وإذا رَمِدَ وأُمِرَ أن لا يغسل عينه، أو انكسر ظفره، وجعل عليه دواء، أو عِلْكا، أو جلدَةَ مَرَارَةٍ، وضرَّهُ نَزْعُهُ، جاز له المسحُ، وإن ضره المسحُ تَرَكَهُ. وَلَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلى النِّيَّة في مسح الخف، والجبيرة، والرأس. ولا يجب إعادة المسح عليها، أي: على الموضوعة عوضاً عن الأولى، والأفضل إعادته، أي: المسح على الثانية، لشبهة البديلة (وإذا رَمِدَ وأُمِرَ)، أي: أمره طبيب مسلم حاذق (أن لا يغسل عينه)، أو غلب على ظنه ضرر الغسل تركه، (أو انكسر ظُفْرُهُ)، أو حصل به داء، (وجعل عليه دواء، أو عِلْكَاً) ليمنع عنه ضرر الماء ونحوه، (أو) جعل عليه (جلدَةَ مَرَارة) ونحوها، (وضره نَزْعُه، جاز له المسحُ للضرورة، ولا يكلف بالغسل للحرج، وإن ضره المسح) لشدة الوجع (تَرَكَهُ)، لأن الضرورة تقدر بقدرها، (وَلا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلى النِّيَّةِ في مَسْحِ الخُفّ)، وقيل: تشترط النية في مسح الخف كالتيمم؛ لأن كل واحد منهما بدل، والأظهر أنه لا يشترط فيه النية، لأنه طهارة بالماء، كالوضوء، إذ هو بعضه فصار مسح الخف (و) مسح (الجبيرة و) مسح (الرأس) سواء في عدم اشتراط النية في مسحها، لأنه طهارة ماء، بخلاف التيمم لقيام الدليل فيه.
And if a person has inflammation of the eye and is ordered not to wash his eye, or his nail is broken and he puts medicine or gum or gallbladder skin on it, and removing it would harm him, he may wipe over it; and if wiping harms him, he leaves it. And intention is not required for wiping over the khuff, the splint, and the head. And it is not obligatory to repeat the wipe over it, i.e. over the one placed in exchange for the first, but it is better to repeat it, i.e. the wipe over the second, due to the resemblance of substitution. 'And if he has inflammation of the eye and is ordered', i.e. ordered by a skilled Muslim doctor, 'not to wash his eye', or his dominant assumption is that washing would harm him, he leaves it. 'Or his nail is broken' or he has a disease, 'and he puts medicine or gum on it' to prevent harm from water and the like, 'or puts gallbladder skin' or similar, 'and removing it would harm him, he may wipe over it due to necessity, and he is not obligated to wash due to hardship; and if wiping harms him' due to severe pain, 'he leaves it', because necessity is measured accordingly. 'And intention is not required in wiping over the khuff', and it is said that intention is required in wiping over the khuff like tayammum because each is a substitute, but the more apparent view is that intention is not required for it, because it is a purification with water, like wudu, since it is part of it. So wiping over the khuff, and wiping over the splint, and wiping over the head are all equal in that intention is not required for wiping them, because they are water purification, contrary to tayammum due to the evidence there.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage covers additional cases where wiping over a covering is permitted, and the ruling on intention (niyyah) for wiping. The commentator first discusses cases of necessity: if one has eye inflammation (ramad) and is advised not to wash the eye, or has a broken nail with medicine, or a wound covered with a protective substance, and removing it would cause harm, then wiping over that covering is allowed instead of washing. However, if wiping itself causes harm, it is left. This follows the principle of necessity (darurah) being measured accordingly. Then the passage addresses the issue of intention for wiping over khuff, jabirah, and the head. There is a view that intention is required because these are substitutes, similar to tayammum. But the relied-upon (azhar) position in the Hanafi school is that intention is not required for any of these wipes, because they are part of the water-based purification of wudu. Unlike tayammum, which is a dry purification and requires intention based on specific evidence. Therefore, wiping over the khuff, jabirah, and head does not require separate intention; it suffices that the intention of wudu covers them.

Key Terms
النية (al-niyyah): Intention: the mental resolve to perform an act of worship for the sake of Allah.
الرَمَد (al-ramad): Inflammation of the eye, a medical condition that may prevent washing with water.
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that permits relaxation of normal rules in order to avoid harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, why is intention not required for wiping over the khuff?
A) Because it is a substitute like tayammum, but evidence shows intention is not needed.
B) Because it is water purification like wudu, of which it is a part.
C) Because khuff is not a valid body part for wudu.
D) Because the Prophet never intended when wiping his khuff.

Key Takeaway
Necessity permits wiping over coverings; for khuff, jabirah, and head, intention is not required because they are water-based purification like wudu.

Mas-h on Bandage and Intention Requirement
Rulings on mas-h over bandage and intention requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for permissible mas-h on a bandage and explain why intention is not required for mas-h on khuff, bandage, and head according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Mas-h on bandage is permissible under necessity; intention is not required because it is purification with water, like wudu.

This passage from Imdad al-Fattah clarifies two related rulings: the permissibility of wiping over a bandage (jabirah) during wudu when removal would cause harm, and the absence of an intention requirement for mas-h on khuff, bandage, and the head. The Hanafi school distinguishes these acts from tayammum, which does require intention. Understanding these rulings prevents unnecessary difficulty and ensures correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage rules: mas-h on a bandage is permissible (mubah) under necessity, and intention (niyyah) is not required for mas-h on khuff, bandage, and head.
The ruling is based on the principle of removing hardship.
2
Examine the conditions for permissibility
List the conditions: the bandage must be applied due to an injury or medical need, and removing it would cause harm or significant difficulty.
Harm can be physical (pain) or fear of slowing healing.
3
Understand the scope of necessity
The passage gives examples: eye inflammation (ramad) where washing is harmful, a broken nail with dressing. If mas-h itself causes harm, it is left entirely.
Necessity is measured by its extent – only what is needed is excused.
4
Analyze the intention requirement
The Hanafi position is that intention is not required for mas-h on khuff, bandage, or head. The proof is that these are purification with water, analogous to wudu, not like tayammum which substitutes for water.
Some Hanafi scholars differed, but the relied-upon (al-azhar) view is no intention needed.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i requires intention for mas-h on khuff, considering it a substitute (badal) like tayammum. The Hanafi response distinguishes: mas-h on khuff is part of wudu, not a separate substitution.
The practical difference: Shafi'i would repeat wudu if intention was omitted; Hanafi would not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَجِبُ إِعَادَةُ الْمَسْحِ عَلَيْهَا، وَالْأَفْضَلُ إِعَادَتُهُ. وَإِذَا رَمِدَ وَأُمِرَ أَنْ لَا يَغْسِلَ عَيْنَهُ، أَوِ انْكَسَرَ ظُفْرُهُ، وَجَعَلَ عَلَيْهِ دَوَاءً، أَوْ عِلْكًا، أَوْ جِلْدَةَ مَرَارَةٍ، وَضَرَّهُ نَزْعُهُ، جَازَ لَهُ الْمَسْحُ، وَإِنْ ضَرَّهُ الْمَسْحُ تَرَكَهُ. وَلَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى النِّيَّةِ فِي مَسْحِ الْخُفِّ، وَالْجَبِيرَةِ، وَالرَّأْسِ.
It is not obligatory to repeat the mas-h upon it, but repeating it is better. And if one has an eye inflammation and is ordered not to wash his eye, or his nail is broken, and he puts upon it medicine, or gum, or gallbladder skin, and removing it would harm him, mas-h is permissible for him. And if mas-h would harm him, he leaves it. And intention is not required for mas-h of the khuff, the bandage, and the head.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh of Nur al-Idah on the issue of mas-h (wiping) over bandages (jabirah) and the requirement of intention. The passage begins by stating that if one places a bandage over a wound and performs mas-h over it during wudu, it is not obligatory to repeat the mas-h when the bandage is replaced, though it is recommended. Then it discusses cases of necessity: if one has eye inflammation (ramad) and a trustworthy Muslim doctor advises not to wash the eye, or if a nail is broken and a dressing is applied, and removing it would cause harm, mas-h over the dressing is permitted. However, if the mas-h itself would cause harm, it is left entirely. The final part addresses the requirement of intention (niyyah) for mas-h over the khuff (leather socks), the bandage, and the head. The Hanafi position is that intention is not required because these are acts of purification with water, similar to wudu, unlike tayammum which requires intention. There is a reported opinion that intention is required for mas-h over khuff, but the relied-upon view (al-azhar) is that it is not. This is based on the principle that necessity is measured by its extent.

Key Terms
جَبِيرَة (jabīrah): A bandage or splint placed over a wound or broken bone.
خُفّ (khuff): Leather socks worn over the feet, permissible to wipe over during wudu.
نِيَّة (niyyah): Intention, the deliberate resolve to perform an act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is intention required for mas-h over the khuff (leather socks)?
A) Yes, because it is a substitute for washing.
B) No, because it is purification with water, like wudu.
C) It is recommended but not obligatory.
D) It is required only if the khuff is impure.

Key Takeaway
Mas-h on bandage is permissible under necessity, and intention is not required for mas-h on khuff, bandage, or head because it is purification with water.

Defining Menstruation (Hayd) in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition and conditions of hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of hayd and identify its conditions (shurut) and pillar (rukn).

Core Idea
Hayd is blood ejected from the womb of a mature woman, not due to illness or pregnancy, before menopause.

This lesson introduces the legal category of hayd (menstruation) and distinguishes it from nifas and istihadah. Understanding the precise definition and conditions is essential because hayd triggers numerous rulings in worship and family law. The text from Imdad al-Fattah lays out the definition, conditions, and the rukn, forming the foundation for all menstrual rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of vaginal bleeding
The text states that three types of blood exit the vagina: hayd (menstrual), nifas (postpartum), and istihadah (chronic). Distinguish them by their source and cause.
Istihadah is bleeding from a vein or illness, not from the womb.
2
Extract the legal definition of hayd
Find the phrase 'دم ينفضه رحم بالغة...' and list the components: blood ejected with force, from the womb, of a mature woman, not due to illness, not during pregnancy, before menopause.
The definition excludes other types of bleeding.
3
List the conditions (shurut) for hayd
The text mentions: reaching suitable age (nine years), not pregnant, minimum purity interval (15 days), and minimum duration (three days).
These conditions must all be present for blood to be considered hayd.
4
Identify the rukn (pillar) of hayd
The rukn is the emergence of the specific blood from the womb.
Without this emergence, the ruling of hayd does not apply.
5
Note the linguistic vs. legal definition
Linguistically, hayd means 'flowing'; legally it is the specific blood described.
The legal definition is more precise and carries legal consequences.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

دم ينفضُهُ رَحِمُ بالغة، لا داءَ بها ولا حَبَلَ، ولم تبلغ سن الإياس. وأقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام، وأوسطه خمسة، وأكثره عشرة ..
وقراءة القرآن، ومسه، والاعتكاف، ودخول المسجد، وطواف الحج والبلوغ. وحقيقته: (دَم يَنْفُضُهُ)، أي: يدفعه بقوة وشدة (رَحِمٌ)، وهو محل تربية الولد من نطفة (بالغةٍ) تسع سنين فما فوقها، (لا دَاءَ بها ) يقتضي خروج دم ،بسببه، ولا حَبَلَ لأن الحبلى فم رحمها منسد بالحبل، كمـا أجــرى الله سبحانه عادته بذلك، فلا يخرج منه شيء، حتى يخرج الولد أو أكثره. (ولم تبلغ سن الإياس) وهـو خمس وخمسون سنة على المفتى به، وهذا تعريفه شرعاً. وأما لغة: فأصله السيلان، يقال: حــاض الوادي، أي: سال. وقال الأزهري الحيض دم يرخيه رحم المرأة بعد بلوغها في أوقات معتادة. ويقال: حاضت المرأة، تحيض، حيضاً، ومحيضاً، ومحاضاً، فهي حائض، بحذف التاء، لأنـه صفـة المؤنث خاصة، فلا يحتاج إلى علامة تأنيث بخلاف قائمة ومسلمة. هذه اللغة الفصيحة المشهورة، وحكى الجوهري» عن «الفراء » أنه يقال أيضاً: حائضة، وله أسماء نظمها بعضهم فقال: [من البحر الطويل] وللحيض أسماء ثلاث وعشرة محيض محاض ثم كيد واعصار

Chapter of Menstruation, Postpartum Bleeding, and Istihadah. Three types exit from the vagina: hayd, nifas, istihadah. ... The reality: blood that the womb of a mature woman ejects, not due to illness or pregnancy, and she has not reached the age of menopause. The minimum of hayd is three days, its average is five, and its maximum is ten.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the chapter on menstruation and related bleeding. The author begins by distinguishing three types of blood exiting the vagina: menstrual blood (hayd) from the uterus, postpartum blood (nifas) after childbirth, and chronic bleeding (istihadah) from other causes. He emphasizes that understanding hayd is crucial because it affects many rulings such as divorce, waiting periods, iddah, lineage, and permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, touching the mushaf, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, and tawaf. The legal definition of hayd is specified: blood ejected with force from the womb of a mature woman (nine years or older), not due to an illness, not during pregnancy (since the womb is closed), and before the age of menopause (55 years according to the relied-upon position). The linguistic root is ‘sailan’ (flowing). The conditions (shurut) for hayd are: reaching appropriate age, not being pregnant, that the intervening period of purity is not less than its minimum, and that it reaches the minimum amount (three days). Its rukn (pillar) is the emergence of the specific blood, which is close to black, sharp-smelling, and causing a burning sensation. The minimum duration of hayd is three days with their nights, the average is five, and the maximum is ten. Some narrate that Abu Hanifa initially said the maximum is fifteen, but the later relied-upon view is ten, based on hadith evidence. Continuous flow throughout the entire period is not required; it suffices for blood to be present at the beginning and end of the period.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood that the uterus of a mature woman ejects forcefully, not due to illness or pregnancy, and before menopause.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic bleeding from the womb due to illness or other causes, not hayd or nifas.
إياس (iyas): Age of menopause, set at 55 years (mufta bihi).
رحم (rahim): The womb, the source of menstrual blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for hayd according to the Hanafi school?
A) The blood comes from the womb.
B) The woman is at least nine years old.
C) The bleeding must be continuous for three full days without any break.
D) She is not pregnant.

Key Takeaway
Hayd is legally defined as specific blood from the womb of a mature woman, subject to conditions; understanding these conditions is essential for correct application of rulings.

The Duration of Menstruation: Minimum, Average, Maximum
Duration of hayd with evidence and ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the minimum, average, and maximum days of hayd and explain the evidence and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The minimum hayd is three days, average five, maximum ten – based on hadith and the final Hanafi consensus.

This lesson focuses on the duration of hayd, a critical element in defining menstruation. The text specifies the limits and mentions an early opinion of Abu Hanifa that the maximum is fifteen days, later retracted to ten. We will examine the hadith evidence and the reasoning that led to the adopted position.

Guided Path
1
Extract the minimum, average, and maximum
From the phrase: 'وأقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام بلياليها، وأوسطه خمسة أيام، وأكثره عشرة' – record these numbers.
The 'average' is the most common duration.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text mentions 'وقيل أكثره خمسة عشر يوماً، وهو قول أبي حنيفة أولاً' – note that Abu Hanifa initially thought 15, then retracted.
The relied-upon position is 10 days.
3
Analyze the hadith evidence
The text quotes: 'أقل الحيض للجارية البكر والثيب ثلاث، وأكثر ما يكون عشرة أيام، فإذا زاد فهي مستحاضة' – this is the proof for the limits.
Multiple chains make the hadith hasan despite individual weakness.
4
Understand the mufta bihi position
The final position of the school is that the maximum is ten days, as held by the Sahibayn and later Hanafi authorities.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
5
Note the rule on continuity
The text states: 'ليس الشرط دوام استمرار الدم في جميع مدته، بل في ابتداء المدة وانتهائها' – it suffices that blood appears at the beginning and end.
Breaks in between still count as part of the period.

دم ينفضُهُ رَحِمُ بالغة، لا داءَ بها ولا حَبَلَ، ولم تبلغ سن الإياس. وأقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام، وأوسطه خمسة، وأكثره عشرة ..
وقراءة القرآن، ومسه، والاعتكاف، ودخول المسجد، وطواف الحج والبلوغ. وحقيقته: (دَم يَنْفُضُهُ)، أي: يدفعه بقوة وشدة (رَحِمٌ)، وهو محل تربية الولد من نطفة (بالغةٍ) تسع سنين فما فوقها، (لا دَاءَ بها ) يقتضي خروج دم ،بسببه، ولا حَبَلَ لأن الحبلى فم رحمها منسد بالحبل، كمـا أجــرى الله سبحانه عادته بذلك، فلا يخرج منه شيء، حتى يخرج الولد أو أكثره. (ولم تبلغ سن الإياس) وهـو خمس وخمسون سنة على المفتى به، وهذا تعريفه شرعاً. وأما لغة: فأصله السيلان، يقال: حــاض الوادي، أي: سال. وقال الأزهري الحيض دم يرخيه رحم المرأة بعد بلوغها في أوقات معتادة. ويقال: حاضت المرأة، تحيض، حيضاً، ومحيضاً، ومحاضاً، فهي حائض، بحذف التاء، لأنـه صفـة المؤنث خاصة، فلا يحتاج إلى علامة تأنيث بخلاف قائمة ومسلمة. هذه اللغة الفصيحة المشهورة، وحكى الجوهري» عن «الفراء » أنه يقال أيضاً: حائضة، وله أسماء نظمها بعضهم فقال: [من البحر الطويل] وللحيض أسماء ثلاث وعشرة محيض محاض ثم كيد واعصار
در اس وضحك ثم طمـــث عـراكــهـا وضيف وأسـنـة نـفـــاس وإكبــــار ويقال: طمس بالسين، وفراك بالفاء أيضاً ، وقيل : سببه أن أمنا حواء عليها السلام حين تناولت من شجرة الخلد ابتلاها الله تعالى بذلك، وبقي في بناتها إلى يوم القيامة وشرطه: بلوغ سن يليق به، وعدم الحبل، وعدم نقصان الطهر فيما بينه عن أقله، وبلوغه نصاباً، وركنه: بروز الدم المخصوص من محله وصفته دم إلى السواد أقرب لذاع كريه الرائحة، وقد يتغير بعارض. وأحكامه كثيرة، أشرنـا إلى بعضها، وسنذكر ما يتيسر بعون الله تعالى ونقول: (وأقل الحيض ثلاثة أيام بلياليها، (وأوسطه خمسة أيام، وأكثره عشرةٌ) بلياليها . وقيل : أكثره خمسة عشر يوماً، وهو قول أبي حنيفة أولاً لقوله : ( أقل الحيض للجارية البكر والثيب ثلاث، وأكثر ما يكون عشرة أيام، فإذا زاد فهي مستحاضة» (١). والأحاديث متعددة الطرق في تقديره بما قلناه. وإن ضعف بعضها، فالتعدد يرفع الضعيف إلى الحسن والمقدرات الشرعية مما لا يدرك بالرأي ، قال : ( الحيض ثلاثةُ أَيَّامٍ وأربعة وخمسة وستة وسبعة وثمانية وتسعة وعشرةٌ، فإذَا جَاوَزَ العَشْرَةَ فهي مُستحاضة ، وليس الشرط دوام استمرار الدم في جميع مدته، بل في ابتداء المدة وانتهائها، فالانقطاع فيما بين ذلك كنزوله،
Chapter of Menstruation, Postpartum Bleeding, and Istihadah. Three types exit from the vagina: hayd, nifas, istihadah. ... The reality: blood that the womb of a mature woman ejects, not due to illness or pregnancy, and she has not reached the age of menopause. The minimum of hayd is three days, its average is five, and its maximum is ten.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling statement from the chapter on menstruation and related bleeding. The author begins by distinguishing three types of blood exiting the vagina: menstrual blood (hayd) from the uterus, postpartum blood (nifas) after childbirth, and chronic bleeding (istihadah) from other causes. He emphasizes that understanding hayd is crucial because it affects many rulings such as divorce, waiting periods, iddah, lineage, and permissibility of intercourse, prayer, fasting, Quran recitation, touching the mushaf, i'tikaf, entering the mosque, and tawaf. The legal definition of hayd is specified: blood ejected with force from the womb of a mature woman (nine years or older), not due to an illness, not during pregnancy (since the womb is closed), and before the age of menopause (55 years according to the relied-upon position). The linguistic root is ‘sailan’ (flowing). The conditions (shurut) for hayd are: reaching appropriate age, not being pregnant, that the intervening period of purity is not less than its minimum, and that it reaches the minimum amount (three days). Its rukn (pillar) is the emergence of the specific blood, which is close to black, sharp-smelling, and causing a burning sensation. The minimum duration of hayd is three days with their nights, the average is five, and the maximum is ten. Some narrate that Abu Hanifa initially said the maximum is fifteen, but the later relied-upon view is ten, based on hadith evidence. Continuous flow throughout the entire period is not required; it suffices for blood to be present at the beginning and end of the period.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Blood that the uterus of a mature woman ejects forcefully, not due to illness or pregnancy, and before menopause.
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding after childbirth.
استحاضة (istihadah): Chronic bleeding from the womb due to illness or other causes, not hayd or nifas.
إياس (iyas): Age of menopause, set at 55 years (mufta bihi).
رحم (rahim): The womb, the source of menstrual blood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the maximum duration of menstruation (hayd) according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) 7 days
B) 10 days
C) 15 days
D) 3 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maximum for hayd is ten days; bleeding beyond that is istihadah, and the earlier opinion of fifteen days is marjuh.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Definition and Duration of Menstruation (Hayd)
Defining hayd linguistically and legally, stating its minimum, middle, and maximum durations with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hayd linguistically and legally, state its minimum and maximum durations in the Hanafi school, identify the proof for these durations, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over the maximum.

Core Idea
Menstruation is legally defined by blood expelled from a mature woman, minimum 3 days, maximum 10, established by prophetic hadith.

This lesson examines the foundational rulings on menstruation (hayd) in the Hanafi school. We begin with the linguistic and legal definitions, then establish the minimum, middle, and maximum durations, and finally analyze the proof and the internal disagreement on the maximum. Understanding these durations is crucial for distinguishing hayd from istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding) and applying the correct rules of purity and worship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions and conditions of hayd
Read the passage to extract the linguistic meaning (flow) and the legal definition: blood expelled by the womb of a mature woman (≥9 years) who is not pregnant, ill, or at menopause. Note the conditions: maturity, absence of pregnancy/illness, age below menopause (55).
The legal definition includes both positive and negative conditions.
2
State the minimum, middle, and maximum durations
From the text: minimum = 3 days with nights, middle = 5 days, maximum = 10 days with nights. Note that intermittent bleeding counts as long as it occurs at the beginning and end of the period.
The middle duration is not a fixed ruling but a usual average.
3
Examine the dalil (proof) for the durations
The passage cites a hadith: 'The minimum of menstruation for a virgin and a non-virgin is three, and its maximum is ten days; if it exceeds, she is mustahadah.' The hadith is transmitted through multiple chains, raising its grade to hasan despite individual weakness. The author notes that such legal estimatives are not based on opinion.
The hadith is from al-Daraqutni and Ibn Adi; it is zanni in both thubut and dalala.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute and the mufta bihi
Abu Hanifa initially held the maximum as 15 days, then retracted to 10. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (Sahibayn) agreed on 10. The later school (including the author) adopts 10 as the relied-upon position. The practical consequence: bleeding beyond 10 days is istihadah, not hayd.
This is an example of a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school resolved by stronger evidence.

Blood that the womb expels, from a mature woman, who has no illness causing blood and is not pregnant, and has not reached the age of menopause. The minimum of menstruation is three days, the middle is five, and the maximum is ten... And its reality: blood that the womb expels forcefully, and the womb is the place of raising the child from a drop, from a mature woman who is nine years or older, who has no illness necessitating blood, and is not pregnant because the mouth of the womb of a pregnant woman is closed by the pregnancy, as Allah Almighty has accustomed, so nothing comes out until the child or most of it comes out. And she has not reached the age of menopause, which is fifty-five years according to the fatwa. This is its legal definition. Linguistically, its root is flow; it is said: 'the valley menstruated' meaning it flowed. Al-Azhari said: Menstruation is blood that the woman's womb releases after her maturity at regular times. It is said: the woman menstruated, she menstruates, menstruation, and she is a menstruating woman, dropping the ta' because it is a description specific to the feminine, so it does not need a feminization marker unlike qa'imah and muslimah. This is the eloquent and well-known language. Al-Jawhari quoted from al-Farra' that it is also said: ha'idah, and it has names that some have organized into verse: [from the long meter] and for menstruation there are thirteen names: mahid, mahad, then kid and i'sar, daras, wadhak, then tamth, 'irakuha, and dayf, and asinnah, nifas, and ikbar. And it is said: tams with sin, and firak with fa', also. And it is said: its reason is that our mother Hawwa' (peace be upon her) when she partook of the tree of immortality, Allah Almighty afflicted her with that, and it remained in her daughters until the Day of Resurrection. Its condition: reaching an age suitable for it, absence of pregnancy, that the interval of purity is not less than its minimum, that it reaches a minimum amount. Its pillar: the emergence of the specific blood from its place, and its description is blood tending towards black, burning, foul-smelling, and it may change due to an external factor. Its rulings are many; we have alluded to some, and we will mention what is facilitated with the help of Allah Almighty and we say: (The minimum of menstruation is three days and their nights, its middle is five days, and its maximum is ten and their nights. And it is said: its maximum is fifteen days, and that was the first opinion of Abu Hanifah because of his saying: 'The minimum of menstruation for a virgin and a non-virgin is three, and its maximum is ten days; if it exceeds, then she is mustahadah.' And the hadiths are numerous in its estimation as we have said. Even if some are weak, multiplicity raises the weak to good, and legal estimatives are not perceived by opinion. He said: 'Menstruation is three days, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine, and ten; if it exceeds ten, then she is mustahadah.' And the condition is not continuous flow of blood throughout its whole duration, but rather at the beginning and end of the duration; interruption in between is like its flow.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Nur al-Idah defines menstruation (hayd) both linguistically and legally. Linguistically, hayd means flow; legally, it is blood expelled by the womb of a woman who has reached maturity (at least nine years), is not pregnant, not ill, and has not reached menopause (fifty-five years). The author then states the three categories of duration: minimum three days (with nights), middle five, maximum ten. He notes that some narrated fifteen days as Abu Hanifa's first opinion, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is ten, supported by multiple hadith chains. The author explains that even if individual hadith are weak, their multiplicity raises them to hasan. He also clarifies that blood does not need to flow continuously; it suffices that it appears at the beginning and end of the period. Intermittent bleeding within the days counts as hayd. This is the Hanafi madhhab's position, and all rules of hayd apply during this time.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-hayd): Blood that the womb expels from a mature woman who is not pregnant, ill, or at menopause, distinguished by its minimum and maximum durations.
الاستحاضة (al-istihāḍah): Bleeding that falls short of 3 days or exceeds 10 days in menstruation, or exceeds 40 days in postpartum.
الطهر (al-ṭuhr): The state of purity between two menstruations; its minimum interval is 15 days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum duration of menstruation (hayd) according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) One day
B) Three days and their nights
C) Five days
D) Ten days

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, hayd has a minimum of three days and a maximum of ten days, established by hadith and accepted by the Sahibayn, with intermittent bleeding counted as part of the duration.
